Qada of Supererogatory Acts: Interpreting 'Wajib' in the Definition
Explaining a definitional wrinkle about qada of nafl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a supererogatory act becomes wajib upon commencement, and explain how the definition of 'wajib' in qada must be reinterpreted as 'al-thabit' to include such acts.

Core Idea
A supererogatory act becomes wajib once started; 'wajib' in qada's definition is 'established' (thabit).

In usul al-fiqh, definitions must be comprehensive. The standard definition of qada as 'delivering the like of a wajib' seems to exclude supererogatory acts, which are not originally wajib. How then can one perform qada of a broken nafl? This passage resolves the tension by reinterpreting 'wajib' as 'al-thabit' (what is established as required at the moment of performance), thereby including acts that have become wajib through commencement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The definition of qada requires delivering the like of a wajib. Supererogatory acts are not originally wajib, so how can their qada fit the definition?
2
State the Hanafi rule on nafl
In the Hanafi school, a supererogatory act becomes wajib once commenced (shuru). This is based on the hadith commanding completion of a started prayer.
This rule is central to Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the interpretive solution
The commentator proposes that 'wajib' in the definition should be understood as 'al-thabit' (established), meaning what is obligatory at the time of performance. Thus, the now-wajib nafl qualifies.
This preserves the original definition while accommodating the exception.
4
Note alternative views
The passage mentions 'there are other aspects,' leaving room for other resolutions. This indicates scholarly flexibility in usul reasoning.
These may include redefining qada itself or excluding naskh issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفل فإنما يقضى إذا لزم بالشروع، وحينئذ لم يبق نفلاً بل صار واجبًا، ولكنه يؤدى مع أنه ليس بواجب، فينبغي أن يراد بقوله: عين الواجب الثابت ليعم النفل هكذا قيل، وفيه وجوه أخر.
As for the supererogatory, it is only made up if it becomes binding by commencement; then it is no longer supererogatory but becomes wajib. Yet it is performed even though it is not wajib. So his saying 'identical wajib' should be interpreted as 'the established wajib' to include supererogatory. Thus it was said. And there are other aspects.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection to the definition of qada: supererogatory acts (nafl) can be made up if one starts them and then breaks them. At that point, they become wajib (obligatory) due to commencement, so their qada fits the definition of 'delivering like of a wajib'. However, the performance (ada) of a supererogatory act is not wajib originally, yet it is still called ada. To solve this, the interpreter suggests that 'wajib' in the definition should be understood as 'al-thabit' (established, i.e., what is required at the moment), so that it covers supererogatory acts that have become binding. Other answers are also possible, as mentioned.

Key Terms
النفل (al-nafl): supererogatory act of worship
الواجب الثابت (al-wajib al-thabit): the established wajib, i.e., what is obligatory at the moment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the passage resolve the inclusion of supererogatory acts in the definition of qada?
A) By redefining 'wajib' to mean 'established'
B) By excluding supererogatory acts from the definition
C) By stating that supererogatory acts are never performed as ada
D) By adding a condition of time

Key Takeaway
The definition of qada must be flexible enough to include supererogatory acts that become wajib through commencement; Hanafi scholars resolve this by interpreting 'wajib' as 'established'.

Abrogation of the General Fidyah Verse and the Exception by Ijma
Discussion of abrogation of fidyah verse and historical stages of fasting obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi view on the abrogation of the fidyah verse and identify the role of ijma in preserving the ruling for the elderly.

Core Idea
A verse can be abrogated in general while its ruling remains for a specific group by ijma.

This passage from Nur al-Anwar examines a key example of naskh (abrogation) in the Quran: the initial verse allowing fidyah for those who could not fast was abrogated by the command to fast in Ramadan. However, the ruling requiring fidyah for the decrepit elderly remains in effect, not by the verse but by the consensus (ijma) of the Companions. This shows how multiple sources (Quran and ijma) interact in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two verses
Pinpoint the initial fidyah verse (Quran 2:184) and the abrogating fasting verse (Quran 2:185). Understand that the later verse abrogates the earlier permissive ruling.
The abrogating verse is فَمَنْ شَهِدَ مِنْكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْهُ.
2
Understand the concept of naskh
Naskh means the later revelation supersedes the earlier ruling. Here, the obligation to fast abrogates the option of fidyah for those who are able.
Hanafis hold that naskh can be established by a clear Quranic text.
3
Recognize the exception by ijma
Despite general abrogation, the ruling of fidyah remains for the decrepit elderly. This is not from the Quran but from the consensus of the Companions (ijma).
The passage explicitly states: 'فوجوب الفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني بإجماع الصحابة'.
4
Analyze the role of ijma
Ijma can preserve a ruling that appears to be abrogated, because the Companions knew that the elderly are not addressed by the abrogating verse. This demonstrates that ijma is an independent source of law.
Hanafi usul: ijma is a primary source that can restrict (takhsis) the effect of naskh.
5
Apply the principle
When encountering a general ruling that seems abrogated, check whether ijma has preserved it for a specific category. This prevents overgeneralization of naskh.
This principle is applied in fiqh to other cases as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهي منسوخة: وحينئذ فوجوب الفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني بإجماع الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين. (القمر) فهي منسوحة إلخ : على هذا معنى الآية، وعلى المطيقين الذين لا عذر لهم أن افطروا فدية، وكان الأغنياء يفطرون ويفدون، ثم نسخ ذلك بقوله تعالى: فَمَنْ شَهِدَ مِنْكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْهُ) (البقرة:١٨٥) ويؤيده ما روي عن ابن عمر وسلمة الأكوع أنها منسوخة، وقرأ بعضهم: ﴿وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ ﴾ (البقرة: ١٨٤)، وجعل "أن تصوموا لكم" معطوفًا على الكلام الأول؛ وهو قوله تعالى: كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ (البقرة:١٨٣) والخير بمعنى البر لا الأخير ويمكن أن يكون معطوفا على قوله : لا يطيقونه، فيكون معناه لا يطيقونه بحسب الظاهر بطريق خير بمعنى اليسر وإن كان ممكنا على طريق العسر، فحينئذٍ الصيام خير لهم، فيكون وجوب الفدية بالنص. (السنبلي) وبين أن يفدي ثم نسخ بدرجات على ما حرّرته في "التفسير الأحمدي".
This is how it is abrogated: At that time, the obligation of fidyah for the decrepit old man is by consensus of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them all. (al-Qamar) 'This is how it is abrogated etc.': According to this, the meaning of the verse is: 'And upon those who are able [to fast] but have no excuse, if they break the fast, then a fidyah' – and the wealthy would break the fast and give fidyah. Then that was abrogated by His statement: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it' (Quran 2:185). This is supported by what is narrated from Ibn 'Umar and Salamah ibn al-Akwa' that it is abrogated. Some read: 'And upon those who are able [to fast]' (Quran 2:184) and considered 'and that you fast is better for you' to be connected to the first part, which is His statement: 'Fasting is prescribed for you' (Quran 2:183), with 'khayr' meaning 'goodness' not 'better'. And it is possible that it is connected to His statement: 'they cannot do it', so its meaning is: they cannot do it apparently by way of ease, even if it is possible by way of hardship; then fasting is better for them, so the obligation of fidyah is by the text. (al-Sanbalī) And he clarified the fidyah, then it was abrogated in stages as I have written in 'al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi'.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage addresses the abrogation (naskh) of the fidyah verse. The author explains that despite the verse being abrogated in general, the ruling of fidyah for the decrepit old man remains in effect by the consensus (ijma) of the Companions. The historical context: initially, those who could fast had a choice; then the verse obligating fasting in Ramadan (2:185) abrogated that choice. The author also discusses variant readings and grammatical possibilities that support the abrogation. Finally, he notes that fidyah was then abrogated in stages, a topic elaborated in his own work al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi.

Key Terms
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling by a later revealed text; here the fidyah verse is abrogated by the fasting verse.
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the Companions that the fidyah ruling remains for the elderly despite abrogation of the general verse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the fidyah obligation remain for the elderly despite the abrogation of the verse?
A) Because the verse was never abrogated
B) By consensus of the Companions (ijma')
C) Because the elderly are not required to fast
D) Because the verse applies to all equally

Key Takeaway
Abrogation of a verse does not automatically nullify its ruling for all; consensus can preserve it for specific categories.

Qada' for Udhiyah: Ihtiyat instead of Qada' after Days of Sacrifice
Analyzing substitution of udhiyah with sadaqah after the days pass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qada' and ihtiyat in Hanafi usul, apply the principle that acts which are ghayr ma'qul cannot be made up by qada', and derive the ruling for udhiyah after its time has passed using the Hanafi usul framework.

Core Idea
A sacrifice cannot be 'made up' because it is not rationally understandable; instead, charity is given as a precaution (ihtiyat).

This passage dives into a subtle usul point: when an act of worship involves destruction (like slaughter of an animal), can it be made up after its time? The Hanafi school says no—qada' requires a rational basis (ma'qul). Since udhiyah is not rationally understandable, the obligation after the days is fulfilled by giving charity as a precaution (ihtiyat). Let's unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its time limit
The act is udhiyah (sacrifice) which must be performed during the days of Eid al-Adha. If someone has a designated sheep and misses the days, what is the obligation?
Focus on the time-bound nature of the act.
2
Recognize the implied objection
Since udhiyah involves slaughtering (destroying) an animal, it seems irrational as an act of worship. Therefore, one might think it cannot be made up by qada' (makeup).
This is the key usul point: qada' is only for acts that are ma'qul al-ma'na (rationally understandable).
3
Understand the Hanafi response: ihtiyat not qada'
The author states that the obligation after the days is to give charity (sadaqah) with the sheep or its value. This is not qada' but ihtiyat (precaution).
Ihtiyat is used when there is doubt about which ruling applies, to ensure the duty is fulfilled.
4
Analyze the two probabilities about the original obligation
The passage suggests two possibilities: either the sacrifice itself is the original obligation, or the charity is the original and sacrifice is a substitute due to hospitality. This ambiguity leads to ihtiyat.
This shows the depth of Hanafi usul in considering multiple possibilities.
5
Derive the final ruling
After the days pass, one must give charity (by value or the sheep itself) as ihtiyat. This is not a makeup but a precautionary fulfillment of the divine command.
Thus, the ruling is that charity is wajib, not as qada' but as ihtiyat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أنواع القضاء أو بعين الشاة إن بقيت حيةً عند فوات أيام التضحية أيضًا للاحتياط كالفدية للصلاة، فهو متعلق بالتصدق تشبيه بالمسألة المتقدمة، وجواب عن سؤال مقدر تقريره أن ما لا يعقل شرعًا لا يكون له أي مشروعا قضاء، وخَلَفٌ عند الفوات والتضحية أي إراقة الدم في أيام النحر: غير معقولة؛ لأنه إتلاف الحيوان فينبغي أن لا يجوز قضاؤها بالتصدق بعين الشاة أو بالقيمة بعد فوات أيامها، فأجاب بأن وجوب التصدق بالقيمة أو بالشاة بعد فوات الأيام للاحتياط لا للقضاء؛ وذلك لأن التضحية في أيامها تحتمل أن تكون أصلاً بنفسها، وتحتمل أن تكون خلفًا بأن يكون التصدق بعين الشاة أو بقيمتها أصلاً، وإنما انتقل إلى التضحية بعارض الضيافة؛ لأن الناس أضياف الله تعالى في هذه الأيام والضيافة إنما تكون بأطيب الطعام، جمع ضيف أو بعين إلخ: معطوف على قوله بقيمة .إلخ (القمر) إن بقيت أي الشاة المعينة للتضحية بالنذر أو بالشراء الصادر من الفقير بنية الأضحية. (القمر) فهو تشبيه إلخ يعني أن وجوب التصدق بالقيمة أو بعين الشاة مشابه بالمسألة المتقدمة، وهو وجوب الفدية في الصلاة حينئذ، فالكاف في قول المصنف كالتصدق إلخ داخل على المشبه، والأولى أن يقال: إن هذا الكاف لمجرد القرآن لا للتشبيه يعني أن كلاً من وجوب الفدية للصلاة، ووجوب التصدق في التضحية من قبيل الاحتياط دون القياس، ولا من قبيل القضاء. (القمر) لأنه إتلاف الحيوان إلخ ولا قربة فيه بل هو تعذيب.(القمر) تحتمل أن تكون إلخ هذا بناء على ما قالوا من أن شكر كل نعمة يكون من جنسها مع بقائها فشكر اللسان باللسان، وشكر المال بصرف عين المال، وأما لو كان شكره إتلافه، فلا يتأدى الشكر بعين المال مع بقائه بل بإتلافه لا يقال لو كان التصدق بالعين أو بالقيمة أصلا لوجب أن يجوز في أيام التضحية؛ لأنا نقول أصالته محتملة موهومة، فلا يجوز أن يصح الموهوم المحتمل مع القدرة على المنصوص وهو التضحية. (القمر) أصلاً بنفسها إلخ: فلم يعتبر هذا الموهوم وهو التصدق في معارضة المنصوص المتيقن به وهو التضحية، فإذا فات المتيقن به لفوات وقته وجب العمل بالموهوم وهو التصدق احتياطاً؛ لاحتمال أصالته مع قيام احتمال نيابته. (السنبلي) بقيمتها أصلاً لأنه هو المشروع في باب المال كما في سائر العبادات.(المحشي) أضياف الله تعالى ولهذا كره الأكل قبل الصلاة ليكون أول ما تناوله من طعام الضيافة. (المحشي) إنما تكون إلخ: هذا الحصر على حسب عادة الكريم. (القمر) وهو عند الله اللحم المذكى المراق منه الدم ليكون أول تناول الناس من طعام الضيافة المكرمة، فما دام كانت الأيام موجودةً، قلنا: إن التضحية أصل برأسها وعملنا بالمنصوص، وإذا فاتت على اعتبار الاحتمال الأول الأيام صرنا إلى الأصل، وقلنا: إن التصدق بعين الشاة أو بالقيمة هو الأصل، فحكمنا به، ثم إذا على اعتبار الاحتمال الثاني الأصل جاء العام الثاني لم ننتقل من هذا الحكم و لم نقل بقضائها على ما كان في العام الأول.
Explanation of the types of qada' - or with the sheep if it remains alive at the passing of the days of sacrifice also for ihtiyat like the fidyah for prayer, so it relates to charity. Analogy to the preceding issue, and answer to an implied question: that which is not rationally understandable in shari'ah cannot have any qada' as valid, and a substitute upon loss, and sacrifice (i.e., shedding blood in the days of slaughter) is not rationally understandable because it is destruction of the animal, so one would think that making it up by giving charity of the sheep or its value after its days have passed is not permissible. So he answered that the obligation of charity by value or by sheep after the days is for ihtiyat, not for qada'; because the sacrifice during its days could be an original obligation by itself, and could be a substitute such that charity by the sheep or its value is the original, and one only transferred to sacrifice due to the occasion of hospitality because people are guests of Allah during these days, and hospitality is only with the best of food...
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the ruling on qada' for udhiyah (sacrificial animal) after the days of sacrifice have passed. The author begins by stating that if one vowed a specific sheep for sacrifice and it remains alive after the days, one is obligated to give charity with that sheep or its value as a precaution (ihtiyat), similar to the fidyah for prayer. This answers an implied question: since slaughtering blood is not rationally understandable as an act of worship (it involves destruction), it seems it cannot be made up. Yet the obligation to give charity after the days is not considered qada' but ihtiyat. The reason is that the sacrifice during its days could either be the original act itself or a substitute for charity. The fact that sacrifice involves slaughtering is due to the hospitality of Allah to His guests during these days, and hospitality requires the best food. Thus, during the days, sacrifice is preferred; after they pass, charity becomes the original duty to fulfill the obligation in the best possible manner. The commentary elaborates that this is based on the principle that gratitude for blessings should be of the same genus while the blessing remains. The text emphasizes that the obligation after the days is not qada but ihtiyat, because qada would require a rational analogy, while here it is based on precautionary reasoning.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed act of worship after its time has passed with the intention of fulfilling the obligation.
احتياط (ihtiyat): A precautionary measure taken when there is doubt about which ruling applies; here, it is the obligation to give charity after the days to ensure the duty is fulfilled.
تضحية (udhiyah): The sacrificial animal slaughtered during the days of Eid al-Adha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling regarding a sheep that was set aside for udhiyah but remains alive after the days of sacrifice?
A) It must be slaughtered as udhiyah even after the days.
B) It must be given in charity as ihtiyat, not as qada'.
C) It is no longer valid and no action is required.
D) It must be sacrificed with the intention of qada'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that acts which are not rationally understandable (ghayr ma'qul) cannot be made up by qada'; instead, the obligation is fulfilled by ihtiyat, as illustrated by the case of udhiyah after its time.

Naskh — Abrogation

Does Permissibility Outlast Obligation? The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Abrogation
Usul dispute on the effect of naskh on jawaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether permissibility (jawaz) remains after abrogation of obligation (wujub) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the fast of Ashura.

Core Idea
When obligation is abrogated, permissibility is also removed; a new text is needed to reestablish it.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar tackles a classic usul dispute: if an act was once commanded (wajib), and that obligation is later abrogated, does the permissibility (jawaz) to perform the act remain? Imam al-Shafi'i argued that it does, citing the fast of Ashura. The Hanafi school insists that permissibility was part of the obligation and is abrogated with it. We will unpack the arguments and the practical consequence (thamarah) in a hadith about oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues
First, does a command establish permissibility at all? Second, when obligation is abrogated, does permissibility remain? The text addresses both.
The first issue is preliminary; the second is the main dispute.
2
Understand the Hanafi position on the first issue
The Hanafi jurists hold that the command itself establishes permissibility (jawaz al-imtithal) for the commanded act. Objections about disliked commanded acts are refuted by distinguishing internal vs. external causes of dislike.
This is foundational for the later argument.
3
Follow the main dispute: abrogation of obligation
The Shafi'i evidence: Ashura was obligatory, then abrogated, but remains recommended. The Hanafi response: Ashura's current permissibility comes from a new text (general recommendation of fasting), not the original command. Analogy to the Israelites' limb-cutting illustrates that abrogation removes both obligation and permissibility.
Key: the container (wujub) contains jawaz; nullify the container, nullify the content.
4
Examine the thamarah (practical consequence)
The oath hadith shows the real-world impact: if permissibility remains after abrogation, the oath's obligation might persist; if not, expiation is required. This illustrates the importance of the usul dispute.
Note the Hanafi view requires a new text for permissibility.
5
Analyze the definitions of jawaz
The passage clarifies that jawaz has multiple meanings. The dispute concerns jawaz as the genus of wajib (no sin in doing it), not the mubah (equal option). This avoids confusion.
Terminological precision is crucial in usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص بيان ثبوت صفة الجواز للمأمور به وعدمه والصحيح عند الفقهاء: أنه تثبت به صفة الجواز للمأمور به وانتفاء الكراهة أي المذهب الصحيح عندنا أنه تثبت بمجرد إيجاد الفعل صفة الجواز للمأمور به، وهو حصول الامتثال على ما كلف به، وإلا يلزم تكليف ما لا يطاق، ثم إذا ظهر الفساد بدليل مستقل بعده يعيده، وأما الحج فقد أداه بهذا الإحرام وفرغ عنه، والأمر بحج صحيح في إيجاد الفعل العام القابل بأمر مبتدأ وعند أبي بكر الرازي لا يثبت بمطلق الأمر انتفاء الكراهة؛ لأن عصر يومه مأمور بالأداء مع أنه مكروه شرعًا، والطواف محدثًا مأمور به مع أنه مكروه شرعًا، قلنا: ذلك الكراهة ليس في نفس المأمور به بل لمعنى خارج وهو التشبيه بعبدة الشمس، وكون الطائف محدثًا، ومثل هذا غير مضر. وإذا عدمت صفة الوجوب للمأمور به لا تبقى صفة الجواز عندنا خلافًا للشافعي أنه إذا نسخ الوجوب الثابت بالأمر فهل تبقى صفة الجواز الذي في ضمنه أم لا، فقال الشافعي له: تبقى صفة الجواز استدلالاً بصوم عاشوراء، فإنه قد كان فرضاً ثم نسخت فرضيته، وبقي استحبابه الآن، وعندنا لا تبقى صفة الجواز الثابت في ضمن الوجوب كما أن قطع الأعضاء الخاطئة كان واجبًا على بني إسرائيل، وقد نسخ منا فرضيته وجوازه، وهكذا القياس. وأما صوم عاشوراء فإنما يثبت جوازه الآن بنص آخر لا بذلك النص الموجب للأداء، وقيل: وفائدة الخلاف بيننا وبينه يظهر في قوله عليه: "من حلف على يمين فرأى غيرها خيرا منها فليكفر يمينه ثم ليأت بالذي هو خير"، على مقسم عليه الجواز الذي في ضمنه إلخ : اعلم أن الجائز يطلق على معان منها: ما لا يمتنع عقلاً، ومنها: ما استوى الأمران أي الفعل والترك، وفيه شرعًا وهو المباح، ومنها ما تعارض الأدلة الشرعية فيه كسور الحمار، فإن بعض الدلائل الشرعية تدل على الطهارة وبعضها على النجاسة ومنها ما لا يمتنع شرعًا أي ما حكم الشارع بعدم الحرج فيه، وهذا الجواز الذي يشمل الواجب والمندوب والمباح وهو جنس الواجب، وفي ضمنه، فإن الواجب عبارة عما الحرج في تركه ولا حرج في فعله وهذا هو الجواز الذي يدعي الشافعية بقائه بعد انتساخ الوجوب، والحنفية عدم بقائه صرح به الإعلام وما قيل إن الذي أريد به في المتنازع فيه الإباحة أي التخيير بين الفعل والترك، فلا تصغ إليه، ثم اعلم أن النزاع بيننا وبين الشافعية إنما هو في ما إذا نسخ الوجوب فقط، وأما إذا نسخ فعل الواجب وكان حكم الناسخ التحريم، فلا يبقى الجواز بالاتفاق. نسخت إلخ : أي بفرضية رمضان على ما في حديث رواه جابر بن سمرة له. لا تبقى صفة إلخ فإن بطلان المتضمن يدل على بطلان ما في ضمنه فالجواز لو ثبت ثبت بدليل آخر أو بالإباحة الأصلية، فإن الأمر يفيد الجواز الذي هو في ضمن الوجوب إلا الجواز الذي في ضمن الندب أو الإباحة، فإذا نسخ الوجوب، ففي هذا الجواز، وأما الجواز الذي هو في ضمن الإباحة أو الندب فهو حكم آخر لابد له من دليل آخر. وأما صوم إلخ: جواب عن دليل الشافعي له. بنص آخر أي بدليل أخر وهو القياس على سائر الصيامات النفلية أو حديث وارد بذلك. أخرجه البخاري في "صحيحه" رقم ٦٢٤٨ ، كتاب الأيمان والنذر ، ومسلم رقم ١٦٥٢، باب من حلف بالات والعزى؛ وابن حبان في "صحيحه" :رقم: ٤٣٤۸ ، ۱۸۹/۱۰ ، والنسائي رقم ۳۷۸٤ باب من حلف على يمين فرأى غيرها خيرًا منها أبو داود رقم: ۳۲۷۷ باب الرجل يكفر قبل أن يحنث والترمذي رقم: ١٥٢٩، باب ما جاء فيمن حلف على يمين فرأى غيرها خيرًا منها ، وأحمد في مسنده رقم ۲۰٦٣٧، عن عبد الرحمن بن سمرة
Section on al-Khass: Statement on the establishment of the attribute of permissibility for the commanded act and its non-establishment. The correct position with the jurists is that the attribute of permissibility is established for the commanded act and the negation of dislikeability, meaning the correct madhhab with us is that merely performing the act establishes the attribute of permissibility for the commanded act, which is achieving compliance with what one is obligated with, otherwise it would lead to an impossible obligation. Then if invalidity appears later by an independent proof, he repeats it. As for Hajj, he has performed it with this ihram and finished it, and the command is for a valid Hajj in performing the act of the general that is possible with a new command. According to Abu Bakr al-Razi, negation of dislikeability is not established by the absolute command, because the afternoon prayer is commanded to be performed even though it is disliked legally, and circumambulation while in a state of minor impurity is commanded even though it is disliked legally. We say: that dislike is not in the commanded act itself but due to an external meaning—resemblance to the worshippers of the sun, and the circumambulator being in a state of minor impurity, and such is not harmful. And when the attribute of obligation is absent from the commanded act, the attribute of permissibility does not remain according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i: when the obligation established by the command is abrogated, does the attribute of permissibility that was implied in it remain or not? Al-Shafi'i said: the attribute of permissibility remains, using as evidence the fast of Ashura, for it was obligatory then its obligatoriness was abrogated, and its recommendation remains now. According to us, the attribute of permissibility that was implied in the obligation does not remain, just as cutting off the offending limbs was obligatory upon the Children of Israel, and its obligatoriness and permissibility have been abrogated from us, and such is the analogy. As for the fast of Ashura, its permissibility is now established by another text, not by that text which necessitated performance. And it is said: The benefit of the dispute between us and him appears in his statement: 'Whoever swears an oath and then sees something better than it, let him expiate his oath and then do what is better.' Concerning the permissibility that is implied in it, etc. Know that 'permissible' is used for several meanings: that which is not impossible rationally; that which has two equal sides—action and inaction—and in legal terms it is the permissible; that in which the legal evidences conflict, like the donkey's urine, for some legal evidences indicate purity and some indicate impurity; and that which is not prohibited legally, i.e., that which the Lawgiver has ruled there is no sin in. This permissibility includes the obligatory, the recommended, and the permissible, and it is the genus of the obligatory, and implied in it, for the obligatory is that for which there is sin in leaving it and no sin in doing it, and this is the permissibility that the Shafi'is claim remains after abrogation of obligation, and the Hanafis claim its non-remains, as stated by al-Allama. And what is said that what is intended in the disputed matter is permission (ibaha) i.e., option between action and inaction, do not listen to it. Then know that the dispute between us and the Shafi'is is only when the obligation alone is abrogated; as for when the act of the obligatory is abrogated and the ruling of the abrogator is prohibition, then permissibility does not remain by consensus. 'Abrogated' etc.: i.e., by the obligation of Ramadan according to the hadith narrated by Jabir ibn Samura. 'Does not remain' etc.: for the nullity of the container indicates the nullity of what is contained. So if permissibility is established, it is established by another proof or by original permissibility (ibaha). For the command indicates the permissibility that is implied in obligation, not the permissibility that is implied in recommendation or permission. So when obligation is abrogated, this permissibility goes away. As for the permissibility that is implied in permission or recommendation, it is another ruling that must have another proof. 'As for the fast' etc.: a response to al-Shafi'i's evidence. 'By another text' i.e., another proof, which is analogy to other supererogatory fasts or a hadith to that effect. It was narrated by al-Bukhari in his Sahih, no. 6248, Book of Oaths and Vows; Muslim no. 1652, Chapter on taking oaths by al-Lat and al-Uzza; Ibn Hibban in his Sahih no. 4348, 10/189; al-Nasa'i no. 3784, Chapter on one who swears an oath and sees something better; Abu Dawud no. 3277, Chapter on a man who expiates before breaking his oath; al-Tirmidhi no. 1529, Chapter on one who swears an oath and sees something better; Ahmad in his Musnad no. 20637, from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Samura.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage from Noor al-Anwar is an usul dispute (ikhtilaf) concerning two interconnected questions: (1) whether the command (amr) itself establishes permissibility (jawaz) for the commanded act, and (2) whether that permissibility remains when the obligation (wujub) is abrogated. The author follows the Hanafi school and argues both positions. First, the text states the correct view of the Hanafi jurists: the mere existence of the command makes the act permissible (jawiz) in the sense that performing it fulfills the obligation (imtithal), otherwise it would be an impossible obligation. Abu Bakr al-Razi objected that some commanded acts (e.g., the afternoon prayer at the time the sun turns, or circumambulation while in a state of minor impurity) are legally disliked (makruh) despite being commanded. The Hanafi response clarifies that the dislike is not in the commanded act itself but due to an external cause (e.g., resemblance to sun‑worshippers) and does not affect the intrinsic permissibility. Thus, the command does establish permissibility for the act itself. The second and more significant dispute is with Imam al-Shafi'i over what happens when the obligation is abrogated: does the permissibility that was implied in the obligation remain? The Shafi'i position, using the fast of Ashura as evidence, claims it does remain, because Ashura was once obligatory and after abrogation its recommendation (and thus permissibility) persisted. The Hanafi position, which the text upholds, is that the permissibility does not remain because the abrogation removes both the obligation and the permissibility that was part of that obligation. The example of cutting off limbs for the Israelites illustrates the point: when the obligation was abrogated, the permissibility was also abrogated entirely. For Ashura, the Hanafis argue that its current permissibility comes from a new text (either general recommendation of fasting or specific hadith), not from the original obligation. The practical consequence (thamarah) of this dispute appears in the hadith: 'Whoever swears an oath and then sees something better, let him expiate and do the better.' On the Hanafi view, if the original commanded oath act had its permissibility abrogated, then the oath is broken and expiation is needed; on the Shafi'i view, the permissibility remains, making the situation different. The passage then provides a detailed analysis of the term 'jawaz' explaining its various meanings, and confirms that the dispute is only about cases where the obligation alone is abrogated (not when it is replaced by prohibition, where permissibility is lost by consensus). Finally, it cites the sources for the oath hadith to ground the discussion in authentic transmission.

Key Terms
جواز (jawaz): Permissibility in the sense of no legal impediment; includes wajib, mandub, and mubah. The text distinguishes between jawaz as the attribute that fulfills the command (imtithal) and jawaz in other senses (rational possibility, equal option, etc.).
وجوب (wujub): Obligation, the legal duty to perform an act; established by a command (amr) unless there is a counter-indication.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling by a later revelation or prophetic instruction. Here, it refers specifically to the abrogation of obligation while the act remains as an optional or recommended act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what happens to the permissibility (jawaz) of a commanded act when its obligation (wujub) is abrogated?
A) The permissibility remains exactly as it was, because the command itself grants ongoing permissibility.
B) The permissibility does not remain; it is removed along with the obligation, and a new text is needed to establish permissibility.
C) The permissibility remains only if the act was originally sunnah, not wajib.
D) The permissibility remains for recommended acts but not for obligatory acts after abrogation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: abrogation of obligation removes both the duty and its attached permissibility; a new text is required to reestablish the act's permissibility.

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
8 min
Can a Sin Be a Cause? Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Zina and Affinity Prohibitions
Dispute on whether zina establishes prohibition of affinity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a prohibited act can still be a legal cause (sabab) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument by identifying the key difference in their usul of causation and incompatibility.

Core Idea
A sin can be a cause for a legal ruling: the Hanafi view on zina establishing affinity prohibitions.

In this usul dispute, we examine whether an act that is inherently sinful (ma'siyah) can serve as a cause (sabab) for a beneficial legal ruling — here, the prohibition of marriage due to affinity (hurmat al-musāhara). The Shafi'i position argues that incompatibility between sin and blessing prevents this. The Hanafi response, grounded in analogy and the Quranic verse on lineage, shows that one opposite can be a cause for another, as seen in zhihar. By understanding this debate, you will sharpen your ability to trace how usul principles shape divergent fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
What is the specific legal question at stake? Determine whether zina (unlawful intercourse) creates the same permanent marriage prohibitions as lawful marriage.
This is a dispute about the legal cause (sabab) of prohibitions of affinity.
2
Extract the Opponent's Argument
Al-Shafi'i argues: because zina is a sin (ma'siyah), it cannot be a cause for a blessing (ni'mah) like the prohibition of affinity, as the Quran (al-Furqan 54) presents affinity as a blessing tied to lawful marriage.
The Shafi'i proof rests on incompatibility (tadādd) between disobedience and blessing.
3
Analyse the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi position: an act can be prohibited in one aspect (e.g., its sinfulness) and permissible or a cause in another (e.g., creating lineage). They cite the example of zhihar, which is sinful yet causes the legal ruling of expiation.
Hanafis also use the Quranic verse to argue that any means of creating lineage—including zina—establishes affinity.
4
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
What is the practical difference? Under Hanafi fiqh, a man who commits zina with a woman cannot marry her mother or daughter forever. Under Shafi'i fiqh, these prohibitions only arise from lawful marriage.
This shows how a usul principle directly impacts individual rulings.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The text presents the Hanafi position as the relied-upon view, supported by the principle that a cause can be established through both lawful and unlawful means. The key is that the cause (lineage) is created, not the sinfulness.
Understanding the usul of causation (sababiyya) is essential for reconciling apparent contradictions in revelation.

Topic of the Specific. Explanation of the categories of disgracefulness. And because the prohibited act is disobedience, so it cannot be valid, due to the opposition between them. It is a conjunction upon his saying: 'a statement of complete disgracefulness,' not upon his saying: 'because the prohibition in requiring disgracefulness is a reality' as the apparent suggests. And it is a proof from the perspective of its rules and effects, just as the first is a proof from the perspective of its prerequisite preceding. A second [proof] for al-Shafi'i and its condition. And the difference between the two approaches is clear, and you have known their answers in what preceded within our expositions. Hence he said: 'The prohibition of affinity is not established by zina' — this is the beginning of al-Shafi'i's branches, a folded premise emerging from his saying: 'so it cannot be valid' i.e., and because the prohibited, whether sensory or legal, cannot be valid in itself, nor a cause for another valid [ruling]...

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafis and al-Shafi'i on whether zina (fornication) establishes the prohibition of marriage due to affinity (hurmat al-musāhara). The author first presents al-Shafi'i's argument: the prohibited act is disobedience, and nothing from disobedience can be valid or cause validity; thus zina cannot be a cause for the blessing of prohibition of affinity. The Hanafi response, detailed in marginal comments, is that an act can be prohibited in one aspect and permissible in another, citing the example of a slave who both obeys and disobeys his master. The Hanafi position, affirmed later, is that zina and its preliminaries (like kissing, touching, lustful looking) do establish prohibition of affinity, because they lead to lineage which is the basis for such prohibitions. The commentary by al-Qamar and others clarifies the logical steps and the use of the Quranic verse (al-Furqan: 54) to show that affinity is a blessing. The Hanafis argue that one opposing thing can be a cause for another, as in zhihar (which is a sin) being a cause for expiation. The passage ends with the rationale that the prohibition is ultimately based on the child created from the water of both parties, making it akin to a part of each, and thus marriage prohibited.

Key Terms
خاص (khāṣṣ): Specific; the topic of this section is about specific prohibitions and their causes.
حرمة المصاهرة (ḥurmat al-muṣāharah): The prohibition of marriage due to affinity, i.e., the permanent prohibition of marriage between certain relatives through marriage (e.g., mother-in-law, daughter-in-law).
نهي (nahy): Prohibition; a command to refrain. The discussion is about the legal implications of a prohibition.
قبح (qubḥ): Disgracefulness or ugliness; a moral attribute that the prohibition indicates about the act.
معصية (maʿṣiyah): Act of disobedience; a sin that contradicts the command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, which of the following establishes the prohibition of marriage due to affinity?
A) Only lawful marriage
B) Zina and its preliminaries such as kissing and touching with desire
C) Only zina, not its preliminaries
D) Neither zina nor lawful marriage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position that a sin can be a cause for a ruling is supported by the principle of general terms (ʿāmm) and the example of zhihar, leading to the ruling that zina establishes prohibitions of affinity.

Specification by Non-Independent Factors: Takhsis or Naskh?
Distinguishing between takhsis and naskh when mukhassis is ghaya, shart, istithna', sifa, or badal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between takhsis and naskh in Hanafi usul by identifying non-independent factors and applying the Hanafi rule for their classification.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, specification by condition or exception is naskh, not takhsis, because initial intention changes.

In legal theory, how we classify specification affects the certainty of the general text. This passage dives into a key dispute: when a general term is restricted by a condition, exception, limit, adjective, or substitution, is that specification (takhsis) or abrogation (naskh)? The Hanafi school holds it is naskh, while the Shafi'i school considers it takhsis. Understanding this difference sharpens your ability to analyze legal language and its epistemological weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five non-independent factors
List the five types of non-independent mukhassis mentioned: ghaya (limit), shart (condition), istithna' (exception), sifa (adjective), and badal al-ba'd (substitution of a part). Recognize that these factors are not separate evidence but are embedded in the text itself.
2
Understand the definitional difference between takhsis and naskh
In takhsis, the speaker intends only part of the general from the beginning; in naskh, the whole is intended initially, then the ruling for some parts is removed. Apply this to the five factors: they remove part of the original meaning, so they are naskh.
Hanafi definition of naskh here is not abrogation by a later text, but removal of scope.
3
Apply the distinction to an example
Examine the Quranic verse 'complete the fast until the night' (al-Baqarah 2:187). The word 'until' (ghaya) restricts the fasting period. According to Hanafis, the original command was for the whole day, but 'until the night' removed the obligation for the night part, hence it is naskh.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
The Shafi'i school defines takhsis broadly as any restriction of the general, regardless of how it is expressed. For them, the same example is takhsis. Recognize the practical consequence: in Hanafi usul, the general remains qat'i until the restriction is applied, while in Shafi'i usul, the general becomes zanni once any mukhassis is present.
5
Note the Hanafi nuance from the commentator
The commentator (al-Muhashshi) states that there is no basis for the previous rigid claim that non-independent factors are never called takhsis. He notes that Hanafi scholars often use 'takhsis' loosely, especially when the specification is delayed or metaphorical. This refinement shows the flexibility of Hanafi terminology in practice.
This nuance does not change the core distinction but clarifies usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا إلخ: أي لا يكون تخصيصا اصطلاحًا إن لم يكن أي المخصص مستقلاً، بل كان التخصيص بغاية إلخ، أما الغاية إلخ، فنحو: ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى اللَّيل (البقرة: ۱۸۷) وأما الشرط فنحو: "أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار" فقصر صدر الكلام على بعض التقادير ، وأما الاستثناء فنحو: جاء في القوم إلا زيدا، وأما الصفة فنحو: "في الإبل السائمة "زكاة، ثم اعلم أنه ليس غير المستقل منحصراً في هذه الأربعة، بل له قسم خامس أيضًا، وهو بدل البعض نحو : جاءني القوم أكثرهم. (القمر) بل نسخا ففي التخصيص إرادة البعض من العام من أول الأمر، وفي النسخ أريد الكل من العام، ثم رفع حكم البعض. (القمر) مطلقًا أي أعم من أن يكون المستقل أو بغيره موصولاً أو غير موصول.(القمر) وكثيرا ما يطلق إلخ كما يقال خص الكتاب بالسنة، وخص بعض الآيات بالبعض مع التراخي. عندنا: فلا وجه للقول السابق لا يسمى تخصيصا بل نسخا.(المحشي)
And similarly etc.: i.e., it is not called takhṣīṣ technically if the mukhṣaṣṣiṣ is not independent, but rather the takhṣīṣ is by a ghāya, etc. As for ghāya, etc., e.g., 'then complete the fast until the night' [al-Baqara 2:187]; as for shart, e.g., 'you are divorced if you enter the house'—it restricts the initial speech to some possibilities; as for istithnā', e.g., 'the people came except Zayd'; as for ṣifa, e.g., 'on grazing camels is zakāt'. Then know that non-independent [mukhṣaṣṣiṣ] is not limited to these four; there is also a fifth type: badal al-ba‘ḍ (substitution of a part), e.g., 'the people came, most of them.' (al-Qamar) Rather it is naskh, for in takhṣīṣ, part of the general is intended from the beginning, while in naskh, the whole is intended initially, then the ruling of some parts is lifted. (al-Qamar) Absolutely, i.e., whether the mukhṣaṣṣiṣ is independent or not, connected or not. (al-Qamar) And often it is called takhṣīṣ, as it is said: 'the Book is specified by the Sunnah,' and some verses are specified by others despite delay. According to us: there is no basis for the previous statement that it is not called takhṣīṣ but naskh. (al-Muḥashshī)
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the Hanafi position that specification by non-independent factors—such as a limiting clause (ghāya), condition (shart), exception (istithnā’), adjective (ṣifa), or substitution of a part (badal al-ba‘ḍ)—is not technically called takhṣīṣ but rather naskh (abrogation). The author provides examples from the Quran (completing fasting until night) and from legal expressions (conditional divorce, exception in statements, zakat on grazing camels). The distinction is crucial: in takhṣīṣ, the speaker intended only part of the general category from the outset; in naskh, the whole general was intended initially but later the ruling for some parts was removed. The Shāfi‘ī school considers all these as takhṣīṣ because they define takhṣīṣ as restricting the general to some of its meanings, regardless of the nature of the mukhṣaṣṣiṣ. The Hanafi view is more precise, as it preserves the original qaṭ‘iyyah of the general when the mukhṣaṣṣiṣ is independent. The passage also notes that the term 'takhṣīṣ' is often used loosely even by Hanafis for delayed specification metaphorically. The final note from the commentator (al-Muḥashshī) argues that there is no valid reason for the earlier statement that it is not called takhṣīṣ but naskh, implying that even delayed specification can be called takhṣīṣ in a broader sense.

Key Terms
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ): Restricting a general term to some of its individuals, where the restriction is intended from the beginning of speech.
نسخ (naskh): The lifting of a previously established ruling by a later text or evidence; here, it is applied when a non-independent factor changes the scope of a general after its initial application.
غاية (ghāya): A limit or end that specifies the extent of a ruling, e.g., 'until the night' in fasting.
بدل البعض (badal al-ba‘ḍ): A substitution of a part that explains or refines the general term, e.g., 'most of them' after 'the people came'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi usul, what distinguishes takhṣīṣ from naskh?
A) Takhṣīṣ is by independent evidence; naskh is by non-independent evidence
B) In takhṣīṣ, the general was intended for all initially; in naskh, it was intended for part initially
C) In takhṣīṣ, part of the general is intended from the beginning; in naskh, the whole is intended initially then some is removed
D) There is no difference; they are synonymous

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, specification by non-independent factors is classified as naskh because the general was originally intended for all individuals.

Interpretation of 'Thumma' in Oath Hadith
Debate on whether 'thumma' indicates order or is metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi usul principle used to reconcile conflicting hadith narrations regarding the word 'thumma' and apply it to the issue of advancing expiation.

Core Idea
To preserve obligation, Hanafis interpret 'thumma' as 'and' using the stronger narration and usul principles.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is over the interpretation of the word 'thumma' (then) in a hadith about expiation for oaths. The Hanafis argue that 'thumma' is used metaphorically to mean 'wa' (and), based on a more frequent narration and the need to preserve the obligatory nature of the command. The Shafi'is maintain that 'thumma' indicates order, allowing expiation before violation. This discussion illustrates how Hanafi usul reconciles conflicting texts by preferring the metaphorical interpretation of particles over that of verbs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What is the Hanafi school's ruling on advancing expiation before violating an oath? State it clearly.
The Hanafi position is that expiation must come after the violation.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school disagrees and what is their ruling? What proof do they cite?
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that monetary expiation can precede the violation.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is the Shafi'i dalil? How do they interpret the word 'thumma' in the hadith?
They rely on a narration with 'thumma' and analogy with expiation after wounding.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi author rebut the Shafi'i argument? What alternative interpretation of 'thumma' is offered and why?
The Hanafi response points to a stronger narration with 'wa', and argues that 'thumma' is metaphorical. The principle: metaphor in a particle is preferable to metaphor in a verb.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of this ikhtilaf? How does it affect the validity of early expiation?
For Hanafis, early expiation is invalid; for Shafi'is, monetary expiation is valid if done before violation.

Discussion of 'thumma'.
Explanation of the divisions of the third division.
[Page 343] And an answer to an implied question, which is: that al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) says permissibility of advancing the expiation with wealth before the violation of the oath, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever swears an oath and then sees something better than it, let him expiate for his oath, then do it.' The doing of the good is a metaphor for violating the oath, and its mention with the word 'thumma' after expiation indicates that the better thing is that advancing the expiation before the oath is permissible. So the author replied to him that the word 'thumma' in this hadith is used metaphorically in the sense of 'wa' (and) in accordance with the reality of the command, as indicated by the other narration, which is his saying (peace be upon him): 'Let him do that which is better, then expiate for his oath.' This necessitates advancing the violation before the expiation. So it is necessary to reconcile between them by making 'thumma' in the first narration mean 'wa', so it is understood that both matters are obligatory, i.e., expiation and violation, without preference of one over the other; then order is understood, which is giving precedence to violation over expiation. The better thing is... (abbreviated version of the hadith about swearing an oath) ... as narrated by al-Tabarani from the hadith of Umm Salama raised to the Prophet, as said by al-Qari in Sharh al-Manar. And Abu Dawud narrated from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Samura who said: The Prophet said to me: 'O 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Samura, if you swear an oath and see something better than it, then expiate for your oath, then come to that which is better.' And what is meant by 'oath' is the thing sworn upon; it is called an oath because of its connection with it. (al-Qamar): 'is used metaphorically' etc. The relationship is that 'wa' is for absolute conjunction, and 'thumma' is for restricted conjunction, so this metaphor is of the type of applying the restricted to mean the absolute. (al-Qamar): 'in accordance with the reality of the command' which is obligation. The explanation is that if we act according to the reality of 'thumma', we cannot act according to the reality of the command, which is his saying 'let him expiate', because expiation before violation is not obligatory by consensus, although it is permissible according to al-Shafi'i, so it would necessitate making the command indicate permissibility and other things, and this is metaphorical. Since someone could say: 'Metaphor in the particle (i.e., 'thumma') is not more appropriate than metaphor in the verb (i.e., the imperative), so let the imperative indicate permissibility, etc., and leave 'thumma' as it is.' The author answered him by saying: The other narration indicates that 'thumma' is in the sense of 'wa', which is what is in the two Sahihs from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Samura said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'O 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Samura, if you swear an oath and see something better than it, then expiate for your oath and come to that which is better.' With this exposition, the text's expression is resolved. And what the commentator mentioned at the end of the text's statement 'the other narration' etc., I did not find it in the available hadith books. Ibn al-Humam said: This wording is not known, as in al-Subh al-Sadiq. Based on this, the commentator needed to reconcile between the two narrations and said what he said and prolonged the speech until boredom. (al-Qamar) Its takhrij has passed.
[Page 344]

Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the interpretation of the word 'thumma' (then) in a hadith about expiation for oaths. The Shafi'i position is that 'thumma' indicates order, so expiation can precede the violation of the oath (hanth). The Hanafi author argues that 'thumma' is used metaphorically to mean 'wa' (and), based on another narration that uses 'wa' instead. The key usul principle here is that when two narrations conflict, the one that preserves the literal meaning of the command (obligation) is preferred. The Hanafi argument uses istihsan and prefers metaphorical interpretation of the particle over metaphorical interpretation of the verb, because particles are not essential parts of speech (they are not subject or predicate). The passage also cites Ibn al-Humam who notes that the 'thumma' narration is not well-attested, so it should be interpreted as 'wa' to align with the majority of narrations. This is a classic example of Hanafi usul methodology: preferring the more frequent and stronger dalil, and using qiyas to reconcile conflicting evidence while preserving the actual obligation of the command.

Key Terms
ثم (thumma): A particle indicating sequential order (tarīb) with delay.
استعارة (isti'ara): Metaphorical usage: applying a word to something other than its original meaning due to a relationship between them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi interpretation of 'thumma' as 'wa' in the oath hadith is based on the stronger narration and the usul principle that metaphorical interpretation of particles is preferable, preserving the obligation of the command.

The Conditional Particle 'Law' in Hanafi Usul
Textual analysis of 'law' and its legal redefinition by juristic custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'law' in conditional divorce and explain how juristic custom (urf) redefines linguistic terms.

Core Idea
Juristic custom transforms 'law' from a past-conditional to a future-conditional in Hanafi fiqh.

This passage examines the Arabic particle 'law' (لو) in the context of conditional divorce. While linguists treat it as a past conditional (indicating a condition that did not occur), Hanafi jurists, based on their custom, use it like 'in' (إن) for future conditions. This shift exemplifies a key usul principle: custom can override original linguistic meaning in legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original linguistic meaning of 'law'
Arabic grammarians view 'law' as denoting a past condition that did not happen, e.g., 'if you had come, I would have honored you' implies you did not come. Philosophers reverse the causation but both agree it is past-oriented.
The text mentions two interpretations: 'non-occurrence of consequence due to non-occurrence of condition' (grammarians) and 'non-occurrence of condition due to non-occurrence of consequence' (philosophers).
2
Recognize the juristic custom (urf) that changes its meaning
In the custom of Hanafi jurists (urf al-fuqaha), 'law' is used like 'in' for future conditions. This custom is strong enough to override the original linguistic sense.
The text explicitly says: 'بل صار بمعنى إن في حق الاستقبال في عرف الفقهاء' (Rather, it has become in the meaning of 'in' for future in the custom of jurists).
3
Note the transmission status
No explicit ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa on this issue is narrated. Only the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) have transmitted this view. The author mentions 'روي عنهما' (narrated from the two) to indicate reliance on their narration.
This highlights that the mufta bihi position is based on Sahibayn's view in the absence of Abu Hanifa's explicit statement.
4
Understand the role of intention
Since 'law' can originally carry both meanings, the husband's intention matters. If he intended conditional meaning, divorce occurs upon fulfillment of the condition (at the end of her life? Actually, 'آخر العمر' likely refers to the end of her life, i.e., at death, but the condition is entry, so it's immediate? The text seems confused. The gloss indicates that if he intended condition, it occurs at the end of life; if time, immediately. This may relate to a different particle. For our lesson, note that intention can affect the ruling.
The text says 'فلو نوى الشرط يقع في آخر العمر' which is problematic. In the gloss, it is explained that if he intends condition, the divorce is suspended until the condition is met; 'آخر العمر' might refer to the end of the wife's life if the condition never occurs? But the standard Hanafi view is that a conditional divorce takes effect upon fulfillment. This ambiguity should be noted.
5
Compare with 'idha ma' (side point from gloss)
The gloss on 'idha ma' illustrates the principle that added letters indicate added meaning. Unlike 'idha', 'idha ma' always retains the sense of requital, so it is not equivalent to 'idha' in all respects.
This is a separate discussion but reinforces the idea that linguistic changes affect legal interpretation.

Discussion of 'law'. 'Law' is for condition. So it is according to what he intended, because the wording can bear both. If he intended condition, it occurs at the end of life; if he intended time, it occurs immediately. But if he intends the end of life, he should not be believed in court according to the two, because he intends leniency for himself and is thus suspect, as said. He answered that the similarity between them is only in conditionality, but he differentiated them by considering that the meaning of requital is not separated from 'idha ma' as it is separated from 'idha'. Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the particle 'law' (if) in conditional divorce statements. The author explains that 'law' is originally used for past conditions indicating that something did not happen because its condition was not met. However, in the custom of jurists (urf al-fuqaha), it has come to be used like 'in' for future conditions. Thus, if a man says to his wife 'You are divorced if you enter the house', it is treated as a conditional divorce (like 'in dakhalti al-dara'), so the divorce only occurs upon entry. Importantly, no explicit ruling is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa on this specific issue; the only transmitted view is from the Sahibayn. Therefore, the ruling depends on the husband's intention: if he intends a conditional meaning, the divorce occurs at the end of his wife's life (or at the time of condition fulfillment? Actually, the text says 'if he intended condition, it occurs at the end of life' which seems contradictory. The gloss clarifies: condition means at the end of life for the wife? Possibly if the condition is 'if I do not divorce you'? Actually, in the 'law' example, the condition is entry. There seems to be confusion. Let's read: 'فهو على ما نوى لأن اللفظ يحتملهما، فلو نوى الشرط يقع في آخر العمر، ولو نوى الظرف يقع في الحال' - this suggests that if he intended 'law' as pure condition (like 'in'), the divorce occurs at the end of life (i.e., after the condition is met? That would be immediate upon entry, not end of life. Possibly the text is discussing the general rule for 'idha'? But the section is on 'law'. There is a misplacement. The commentary includes a long gloss about 'idha ma' which actually belongs to the previous section. Due to OCR confusion, we must interpret the text as it appears. The ruling for 'law' according to the narration: it is like 'in' for future, so divorce occurs conditionally upon entry, but the intention matters. If the husband intended 'law' in its original past sense, it might not be conditional? The author says 'It is according to what he intended'. The gloss on 'idha ma' is a correction of a statement made in the previous lesson. The passage concludes that the reason the author mentioned 'narrated from the two' is because there is no explicit text from Abu Hanifa on this issue. This highlights the principle that a ruling's authority weight depends on its transmission. The passage also notes the subtlety that 'law' is considered a conditional particle even though it originally indicates a negated past condition; this is based on juristic custom.

Key Terms
لو (law): A conditional particle originally indicating a past condition that did not occur. In juristic custom, used like 'in' for future conditions.
إذا ما (idha ma): A variant of 'idha' which, unlike 'idha', always retains the meaning of requital (jazā'), meaning it cannot be interpreted as a pure conditional without a sense of immediate consequence.
عرف الفقهاء (urf al-fuqaha): The custom or practice of jurists, which serves as a source of legal interpretation in Hanafi usul.

Key Takeaway
Juristic custom (urf) can transform the meaning of a linguistic particle, as seen with 'law' shifting from past to future conditional, illustrating a foundational Hanafi usul principle.

Defining Muqtada and Mahdhuf
Defining muqtada and mahdhuf and the initial rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define muqtada and mahdhuf and explain the initial rule that distinguishes them based on change in wording.

Core Idea
Muqtada is necessary for textual validity; mahdhuf is for brevity; the change rule is proposed but problematic.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the difference between muqtada al-nass (textual implication) and mahdhuf (deletion) is essential for correct istinbat. This lesson explores the definitions and an early attempt to distinguish them by whether the wording changes when the omitted element is supplied.

Guided Path
1
Define the two terms
From the text, extract the definitions: muqtada is an implicit meaning necessarily required for the text's validity; mahdhuf is a word omitted for brevity, indicated by context.
The text says muqtada is shar‘ī and mahdhuf is linguistic.
2
Identify the initial rule
The text mentions two rules: no change when muqtada appears, change when deletion appears.
These are the 'qā‘idatān'.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the example of 'Strike the stone' and 'Free your slave for me for a thousand' to see how they are used to test the rules.
4
Recognize the problem
The text shows that these examples actually refute the rules, leading to a need for a better distinction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[فتح الغفار ٢٣٢] والظاهر إلخ فإنّ إيراد المثال من النصوص أولى . (القمر) مقتضى : قيل: إنّ كون أصل للتصرفات من الإعتاق وغيره، والأصل لا يثبت اقتضاء، فتأمل فيه. (القمر) أعتق عبدك: أي عن كفّارة يميني مثلاً. (القمر)
Explanation of textual implication. Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division. And the i‘rāb of 'al-qaryah' changes from accusative to genitive. But the two rules are refuted by His statement: 'So We said, Strike with your staff the stone, and there gushed forth from it twelve springs'—for if one supplies [the omitted] 'so he struck and the stone split, and it gushed forth,' the remaining speech does not change by supplying it, even though it is a deletion; and by His statement: 'Free your slave for me for a thousand'—for if one supplies the sale and says 'Sell your slave for me and be my agent for emancipation,' then the speech changes, even though it is an implied necessity [muqtada]; that is, what the text necessitates. And for this it was said: the difference between them is that the muqtada is shar‘ī and the deletion is linguistic, and similar examples. 'And it changes' etc. because before appearing it was in the accusative as a direct object, and after appearing it becomes genitive by annexation. (al-Qamar) The two rules: first, that no change occurs when the muqtada appears; second, that change occurs when the deletion appears. (al-Qamar) By His statement etc. this refutes the second rule. (al-Qamar) 'The speech does not change' said the greatest scholar, i.e., our master ‘Abd al-Salām al-A‘ẓamī, to him: that there is change here, because the gushing forth was dependent on the command to strike with the staff before appearing and became dependent on the splitting after appearing. And therein is that such change also occurs in the muqtada when it appears. Do you not see that emancipation in the famous example of the muqtada from the shar‘ī matters—i.e., His statement: 'Free your slave for me for a thousand'—is not dependent on anything, and after the muqtada appears, if it is said: 'Sell your slave for me and be my agent for emancipation,' emancipation becomes dependent on the sale. Thus it was said, so understand. (al-Qamar) 'And by his statement' is conjoined to 'by His statement etc.' and this refutes the first rule. (al-Qamar) 'And for this': i.e., because of the invalidity of the distinction that the author made between muqtada and deletion, it was said that etc. (al-Qamar) 'That the muqtada' etc.: Know that many uṣūliyyūn have considered the deletion as part of the muqtada, and they define muqtada as that which makes the unspoken spoken to correct the spoken, by shar‘ or reason or language. And some distinguish that the deletion is a concept whose affirmation changes the spoken, and the muqtada is a concept whose affirmation does not change the spoken, etc. In al-Talwīḥ it is said: There is discussion in this distinction. For if what is meant by the distinction between deletion and muqtada is the existence or non-existence of change, then there is no change in like His statement: 'so they struck and it gushed forth' (implied), and His statement in the story: 'Send me' Joseph—i.e., they sent him and he came to him and said 'O Joseph'—and many similar examples occur in deletions. And if what is meant is that the absence of change is necessary in muqtada but not necessary in deletion, then this does not distinguish deletions that have no change from muqtada. Thus in al-Talwīḥ. And it is said in some glosses: The intent in deletion is the restricted meanings from which the suppositions are derived, and in muqtada the necessary absolute meanings. And he said: This is a valid custom in both. (al-Sanbalī) 'Shar‘ī' i.e., established by shar‘ not language. (al-Qamar) 'Linguistic': For the deletion is what is removed from speech as an abbreviation due to the remaining [speech] indicating it, so it is established linguistically. (al-Qamar) 'Language'. And it was said: Both the muqtadī and the muqtadā are intended in the implication, contrary to the deletion, for what is intended therein is only the deletion itself. In short, the deletion is in the status of uttered speech, not devoid of 'ibārah, ishārah, dalālah, and iqtiḍā', and it is not a division separate from the four. And its example: the command to emancipate for expiation necessitates [muqtadī] ownership without mentioning it, and the apparent meaning is that the command to emancipate is His statement: 'So the freeing of a slave' [Sūrah al-Nisā’ 4:92]; for it necessitates ownership that is not mentioned, as if He said: 'So the freeing of a slave owned by you.' For freeing a free person or another's slave is not valid. So 'freeing a slave' is the muqtadī, and 'owned by you' is the muqtadā. And its ruling—ownership—is established by the muqtadā which is established by the muqtadī. And it is said: What is meant by it is His statement: 'Free your slave' by the command to free. And it is said: And it is said: The verbal structure indicates the muqtadā by a necessary implication; for the meaning of the structure is not valid without it, contrary to the deletion, for there is a supposed word indicating its meaning, and the structure does not indicate it except that that word is understood by the indicative context. And this supposed [word] is like uttered [word] in generality and specificity and other matters. (al-Qamar) 'Both are intended' etc., as in his statement: 'Free your slave for me for a thousand'—emancipation and transfer of ownership are both intended by the commander. (al-Qamar) 'Only', i.e., not the explicitly mentioned, as in His statement: 'And ask the village' [Sūrah Yūsuf 12:82], for what is intended in the asking is the inhabitants, not the village. And one could say: This is not general in all cases. Do you not see that the deletion can be intended together with what is mentioned, as in His statement: 'So We said, Strike with your staff the stone'? (al-Qamar) 'In short' etc.: Repelling a presumed objection, the gist being that when the deletion was excluded from the muqtada, a fifth division appeared beyond the four mentioned, but no one has said that. (al-Qamar) 'Not devoid' i.e., in indicating the meaning. (al-Qamar) 'And it is not a division' etc.: For we mean by the word indicating the meaning in the context of division the word either literally or suppositionally, and the deletion is a word suppositionally. (al-Qamar) 'A division separate' etc., lest the divisions become five, and whoever held this had a false illusion; we mean by 'word indicating the meaning' in the context of division the word either literally or suppositionally. So everything that is deleted, though not uttered, is established linguistically, so it is in the status of uttered speech. Thus the spoken word indicates the deleted word, then the word [the deleted] indicates its meaning by one of these four divisions. So the division into four is the division of a word's indication of a meaning. As for a word's indication of another word, it is not from the chapter of a word indicating a meaning. So understand. This is summary of what is in al-Tawḍīḥ. 'And He did not mention it' i.e., Allah did not mention ownership. [Fatḥ al-Ghaffār 232] 'And the apparent' etc.: For presenting the example from the texts is better. (al-Qamar) 'Muqtadā': It is said: that it is a principle for transactions like emancipation and other things, and the principle does not establish implication, so reflect on it. (al-Qamar) 'Free your slave' i.e., for my oath of expiation, etc. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion in uṣūl al-fiqh concerning the concept of 'muqtadā al-naṣṣ' (textual implication) and its distinction from 'al-maḥdhūf' (the deleted element). The text begins by noting a change in i‘rāb from the example of 'al-qaryah' but then focuses on two rules: (1) that when a muqtadā is supplied, the wording does not change, and (2) when a deletion is supplied, the wording does change. These rules are then challenged by examples from the Qur’an. The first counterexample is from Sūrah al-Baqarah 2:60: 'Strike the stone' — if we supply the verbs 'so he struck and it split', the remaining speech (the gushing out) does not change. This refutes the second rule, since the supplied part is a deletion yet no change occurs. The second counterexample is the command 'Free your slave for me for a thousand' — if we supply the contract of sale, the speech changes because the command now becomes a sale and agency. This refutes the first rule, because the supplied part is a muqtadā yet change occurs. Thus the attempted distinction based on change fails. The commentator then discusses alternative distinctions: some say muqtadā is shar‘ī (established by revelation) while deletion is linguistic; others say both muqtadā and muqtadā are intended in the implication, but in deletion only the deleted word is intended. The text ultimately affirms that the deletion is in the status of pronounced speech and is not a separate division beyond the four types of textual indication ('ibārah, ishārah, dalālah, iqtiḍā'). An example is given from Sūrah al-Nisā’ 4:92 ('The freeing of a slave'), which necessitates ownership of the slave (the muqtadā) without which the command cannot be fulfilled. The commentary from al-Talwīḥ and al-Tawḍīḥ is explained to clarify these points.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtaḍā): The concept that is tacitly required by the text's wording for the text to be valid and meaningful; its addition does not inherently change the wording.
المحذوف (al-maḥdhūf): A word or phrase omitted from the speech for brevity, which the context indicates; its restoration often leads to a change in the wording or structure.
اقتضاء النص (iqtiḍā’ al-naṣṣ): The implication of the text, i.e., a meaning that is necessarily required by the wording to make the utterance legally or rationally sound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the initial rule presented in the passage, what is supposed to happen when a muqtada is made explicit?
A) The wording changes.
B) The wording does not change.
C) The ruling is abrogated.
D) The text becomes ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The attempted distinction between muqtada and mahdhuf based on change in wording is refuted by Qur’anic examples, requiring a more robust criterion.

Naskh — Abrogation

Critique and Resolution: Integrative View
Critical evaluation and final integration of mahdhuf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the refutation of the change-based distinction, identify the alternative views, and describe the final Hanafi position that mahdhuf is subsumed under the four textual indications.

Core Idea
After critique, the accepted view is that mahdhuf is like pronounced speech and not a separate division.

This lesson continues the discussion by examining how the initial distinction fails and exploring alternative criteria. The final resolution integrates mahdhuf into the four categories of textual indication.

Guided Path
1
Recap the counterexamples
Recall the two counterexamples that refuted the rules: the deletion in the stone example and the muqtada in the slave example.
2
Analyze the critique from al-Talwīḥ
The text quotes al-Talwīḥ pointing out that if change is the criterion, many deletions do not change wording, so the distinction collapses.
3
Consider alternative distinctions
Explore the suggested differences: muqtada is shar‘ī (by revelation) vs deletion is linguistic; or that both muqtadī and muqtadā are intended in muqtada, but only the deleted is intended in mahdhuf.
4
Understand the final position
The text concludes that mahdhuf is in the status of uttered speech and falls under one of the four divisions of textual indication (ibarah, isharah, dalalah, iqtida). It is not a fifth division.
This is the accepted Hanafi position.

بيان اقتضاء النص بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع ويتغيّر إعراب القرية من النصب إلى الجرّ. ولكن تنتقض القاعدتان بقوله تعالى: فقلنا اضْرِبْ بِعَصَاكَ الْحَجَرَ فَانْفَجَرَتْ مِنْهُ اثْنَتَا عَشْرَةَ عَيْنا فإنه إن قدّر قوله: "فضرب فانشق الحجر فانفجرت" لا يتغير الكلام الباقي بتقديره مع أنه محذوف وبقوله: "أعتق عبدك عني بألف"، فإنّه إن قدر البيع ويقال: "بع عبدك عني وكن وكيلي بالإعتاق"، فإنه يتغير الكلام حينئذ مع أنه مقتضى؛ أي الذي هو مقتضى النص؛ ولهذا قيل: إن الفرق بينهما أن المقتضى شرعي والمحذوف لغوي وأمثاله. ويتغير إلخ لأنه قبل الظهور كان منصوبا بالمفعولية وبعد الظهور صار محرورًا بالإضافة. (القمر) القاعدتان الأولى أنه لا يقع التغيّر عند ظهور المقتضى. والثانية أنه يقع التغيّر عند ظهور المحذوف. (القمر) بقوله تعالى إلخ هذا نقض للقاعدة الثانية. (القمر) لا يتغير الكلام قال أعظم العلماء أي مولانا عبد السلام الأعظمي له: إنه تغيّر الكلام ههنا؛ لأنّ الانفجار كان مرتبا على الأمر بالضرب بالعصا قبل الظهور وصار مرتبًا على الانشقاق بعد الظهور وفيه أنّ مثل هذا التغيير يتحقق في المقتضى أيضًا عند ظهوره، ألا ترى أن الإعتاق في المثال المشهور للمقتضى من الشرعيات أي قوله : أعتق" عبدك عني بألف غير مرتب على شيء، وبعد ظهور المقتضى إذا قيل: "بع عبدك عني وكن وكيلي بالإعتاق" صار الإعتاق مرتبًا على البيع. كذا قيل، فافهم. (القمر) وبقوله معطوف على قوله بقوله تعالى إلخ" وهذا نقض للقاعدة الأولى (القمر) ولهذا: أي لأجل بطلان الفرق الذي ذكره المصنف له بين المقتضى والمحذوف قيل: إنّ إلخ. (القمر) أن المقتضى إلخ: اعلم أنّ كثيرًا من الأصوليين جعلوا المحذوف من المقتضى، وفسروا المقتضى بما يجعل غير المنطوق منطوقا تصحيحا للمنطوق شرعًا أو عقلاً أو لغةً، وبعضهم فرّقوا بأنّ المحذوف مفهوم يغيّر إثباته المنطوق، والمقتضي مفهوم لا يغير إثباته المنطوق إلخ. قال في "التلويح": في هذا الفرق بحث، فإن أريد بوجه الفرق بين المحذوف والمقتضى وجود التغيّر وعدمه فلا تغير في مثل قوله تعالى فضربوا فانفجرت، وقوله تعالى حكايةً: "فأرسلون" يوسف عليه أي أرسلوه فأتاه وقال له: يا يوسف، ومثل هذا كثير في المحذوف. وإن أريد أن عدم التغير لازم في المقتضى وليس بلازم في المحذوف لم يميّز المحذوف الذي لا تغيّر فيه عن المقتضى، كذا في "التلويح". وقال في بعض الحواشي: إنّ المقصود في المحذوف المعاني المقيدة التي يستفاد المقدرات، وفي المقتضى المعاني الضرورية المطلقة. وقال: هذا عرف صحيح فيهما (السنبلي شرعي أي ثابت شرعًا لا لغةً. (القمر) لغوي: فإن المحذوف هو ما أسقط من الكلام اختصارًا لدلالة الباقي عليه فكان ثابتا لغة (القمر) لغة.) وقيل: إنّ المقتضي والمقتضى كلاهما يرادان في الاقتضاء بخلاف المحذوف؛ فإنّ المراد فيه المحذوف لا غير. وبالجملة فالمحذوف في حكم الملفوظ لا يخلو عن العبارة والإشارة والدلالة ،والاقتضاء، وليس قسمًا خارجًا عن الأربعة. ومثاله الأمر بالتحرير للتكفير مقتض للملك ولم يذكره والظاهر أن الأمر بالتحرير هو قوله تعالى: فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ ؛ فإنه مقتض للملك الغير المذكور، فكأنه قال: "فتحرير رقبة مملوكة لكم"؛ فإنّ إعتاق الحرّ وعبد الغير لا يصح؛ فتحرير رقبة مقتض ومملوكة لكم مقتضى. وحكمه وهو الملك ثابت بالمقتضى الذي هو ثابت بالمقتضي، وقيل: المراد به قوله: "أعتق عبدك بالأمر بالتحرير وقيل :إلخ وقيل: إن النظم دال على المقتضى دلالة التزامية؛ فإنّه لا يصح مدلول النظم بدونه بخلاف المحذوف؛ فإن هناك لفظا مقدرًا دالا على معناه، وليس النظم دالا عليه إلّا أنّ ذلك اللفظ يفهم بالقرينة الدالة. وهذا المقدر كالملفوظ في العموم والخصوص وغيرهما. (القمر) كلاهما يرادان إلخ كما في قوله: "أعتق عبدك عني بألف" يكون الإعتاق والتمليك مقصودين للآمر (القمر) لا غير أي لا المصرح كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاسْأَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ﴾ فإن المراد في السؤال هو الأهل دون القرية ولقائل أن يقول: إن هذا ليس عاما لجميع المواد، ألا ترى أن المحذوف قد يكون مرادا مع المذكور، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَقُلْنَا اضْرِبْ بِعَصَاكَ الْحَجَرَ﴾ (القمر) وبالجملة إلخ: دفع دخل مقدّر تقريره: أن المحذوف لما خرج من المقتضى فقد وجد قسم خامس سوى الأربعة المذكورة، ولم يقل به أحد. (القمر) لا يخلو أي في الدلالة على المعنى (القمر) وليس قسما إلخ فإن مرادنا باللفظ الدال على المعنى في مورد القسمة اللفظ إمّا حقيقة أو تقديرا، والمحذوف لفظ تقديراً. (القمر) قسمًا خارجًا إلخ ليلزم كون الأقسام خمسة، ومن ذهب إلى هذا فقد وهم وهما باطلاً؛ مرادنا باللفظ الدال على المعنى في مورد القسمة اللفظ إما حقيقةً وإما تقديرًا ، فكل ما هو محذوف وإن كان غير ملفوظ لكنه ثابت لغةً، فإنّه في حكم الملفوظ، فيكون اللفظ المنطوق دالا على اللفظ المحذوف، ثم اللفظ دل المحذوف على معناه بأحد هذه الأقسام الأربع؛ فالدلالة المنقسمة علي الأربع دلالة اللفظ على المعنى، أما دلالة اللفظ على لفظ آخر؛ فليس من باب دلالة اللفظ على معنى فافهم. هذا ملخص ما في "التوضيح". ولم يذكره أي لم يذكر الله تعالى الملك. [فتح الغفار ٢٣٢] والظاهر إلخ فإنّ إيراد المثال من النصوص أولى . (القمر) مقتضى : قيل: إنّ كون أصل للتصرفات من الإعتاق وغيره، والأصل لا يثبت اقتضاء، فتأمل فيه.
